## Chapter: Physics for Solid-State Applications



### Introduction



In this chapter, we will explore the concept of effective mass and its role in equilibrium in solid-state applications. Effective mass is a fundamental concept in solid-state physics that describes the behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice. It is a measure of the inertia of an electron in a solid, and it plays a crucial role in determining the electronic properties of materials.



We will begin by discussing the concept of effective mass and how it is related to the mass of a free electron. We will then delve into the mathematical formulation of effective mass and its significance in the band structure of materials. This will be followed by a discussion on the different types of effective mass, such as isotropic and anisotropic effective mass, and their implications in solid-state applications.



Next, we will explore the concept of equilibrium in solid-state systems and how effective mass plays a crucial role in maintaining equilibrium. We will discuss the conditions for equilibrium and how effective mass affects the transport of electrons in a crystal lattice. This will be followed by a discussion on the relationship between effective mass and the Fermi energy, and how it affects the electronic properties of materials.



Finally, we will conclude this chapter by discussing the applications of effective mass in various solid-state devices, such as transistors, solar cells, and LEDs. We will also touch upon the limitations of effective mass and its role in future advancements in solid-state technology.



Overall, this chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of effective mass and its significance in equilibrium in solid-state applications. It will serve as a foundation for further exploration of solid-state physics and its applications in modern technology. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section 4.1: Effective Mass



Effective mass is a fundamental concept in solid-state physics that describes the behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice. It is a measure of the inertia of an electron in a solid, and it plays a crucial role in determining the electronic properties of materials.



#### 4.1a: Definition of Effective Mass



In solid-state physics, a particle's effective mass (often denoted <math display="inline">m^*</math>) is the mass that it "seems" to have when responding to forces, or the mass that it seems to have when interacting with other identical particles in a thermal distribution. It is a measure of the electron's response to an external force, and it is related to the mass of a free electron, <math display="inline">m_e</math>. The effective mass can be thought of as the mass of an electron in a crystal lattice, taking into account the interactions with the surrounding atoms.



The effective mass can be calculated using the band structure of a material. In a periodic potential, the movement of particles over long distances larger than the lattice spacing can be very different from their motion in a vacuum. The effective mass is a quantity that is used to simplify band structures by modeling the behavior of a free particle with that mass. For some purposes and some materials, the effective mass can be considered to be a simple constant of a material. In general, however, the value of effective mass depends on the purpose for which it is used, and can vary depending on a number of factors.



For electrons or electron holes in a solid, the effective mass is usually stated as a factor multiplying the rest mass of an electron, <math display="inline">m_e</math> (9.11  10<sup>31</sup> kg). This factor is usually in the range 0.01 to 10, but can be lower or higherfor example, reaching 1,000 in exotic heavy fermion materials, or anywhere from zero to infinity (depending on definition) in graphene. As it simplifies the more general band theory, the electronic effective mass can be seen as an important basic parameter that influences measurable properties of a solid, including everything from the efficiency of a solar cell to the speed of an integrated circuit.



Effective mass can also be anisotropic, meaning it varies in different directions in the crystal lattice. This is often the case in materials with complex crystal structures, and it can have significant effects on the electronic properties of the material. In some cases, the effective mass can even become negative, leading to unusual behavior of electrons in the material.



In summary, effective mass is a crucial concept in solid-state physics that describes the behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice. It is a measure of the electron's response to an external force and is related to the mass of a free electron. The value of effective mass can vary depending on the material and its purpose, and it can have significant effects on the electronic properties of a material. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section 4.1: Effective Mass



Effective mass is a fundamental concept in solid-state physics that describes the behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice. It is a measure of the inertia of an electron in a solid, and it plays a crucial role in determining the electronic properties of materials.



#### 4.1a: Definition of Effective Mass



In solid-state physics, a particle's effective mass (often denoted <math display="inline">m^*</math>) is the mass that it "seems" to have when responding to forces, or the mass that it seems to have when interacting with other identical particles in a thermal distribution. It is a measure of the electron's response to an external force, and it is related to the mass of a free electron, <math display="inline">m_e</math>. The effective mass can be thought of as the mass of an electron in a crystal lattice, taking into account the interactions with the surrounding atoms.



The effective mass can be calculated using the band structure of a material. In a periodic potential, the movement of particles over long distances larger than the lattice spacing can be very different from their motion in a vacuum. The effective mass is a quantity that is used to simplify band structures by modeling the behavior of a free particle with that mass. For some purposes and some materials, the effective mass can be considered to be a simple constant of a material. In general, however, the value of effective mass depends on the purpose for which it is used, and can vary depending on a number of factors.



For electrons or electron holes in a solid, the effective mass is usually stated as a factor multiplying the rest mass of an electron, <math display="inline">m_e</math> (9.11  10<sup>31</sup> kg). This factor is usually in the range 0.01 to 10, but can be lower or higherfor example, reaching 1,000 in exotic heavy fermion materials, or anywhere from zero to infinity (depending on the material and the specific band being considered).



#### 4.1b: Effective Mass in Different Bands



The effective mass of an electron can vary depending on the band in which it is located. In a solid, electrons can occupy different energy bands, each with its own unique band structure. The effective mass in each band can be different, and this can have a significant impact on the electronic properties of the material.



For example, in semiconductors, the effective mass of electrons in the conduction band is typically smaller than the effective mass of holes in the valence band. This is due to the fact that the conduction band is formed by the overlap of atomic orbitals, which leads to a more delocalized electron and a smaller effective mass. On the other hand, the valence band is formed by the bonding of atoms, which results in a more localized hole and a larger effective mass.



In metals, the effective mass can also vary depending on the band. In the case of heavy fermion materials, the effective mass in the conduction band can be significantly larger than the effective mass in the valence band. This is due to the strong electron-electron interactions in these materials, which can lead to a renormalization of the effective mass.



Understanding the effective mass in different bands is crucial for predicting and explaining the electronic properties of materials. It allows us to better understand the behavior of electrons in a solid and how they contribute to the overall properties of the material. In the next section, we will explore how the effective mass can be experimentally determined and its implications for equilibrium in solid-state systems.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section 4.1: Effective Mass



Effective mass is a fundamental concept in solid-state physics that describes the behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice. It is a measure of the inertia of an electron in a solid, and it plays a crucial role in determining the electronic properties of materials.



#### 4.1a: Definition of Effective Mass



In solid-state physics, a particle's effective mass (often denoted $m^*$) is the mass that it "seems" to have when responding to forces, or the mass that it seems to have when interacting with other identical particles in a thermal distribution. It is a measure of the electron's response to an external force, and it is related to the mass of a free electron, $m_e$. The effective mass can be thought of as the mass of an electron in a crystal lattice, taking into account the interactions with the surrounding atoms.



The effective mass can be calculated using the band structure of a material. In a periodic potential, the movement of particles over long distances larger than the lattice spacing can be very different from their motion in a vacuum. The effective mass is a quantity that is used to simplify band structures by modeling the behavior of a free particle with that mass. For some purposes and some materials, the effective mass can be considered to be a simple constant of a material. In general, however, the value of effective mass depends on the purpose for which it is used, and can vary depending on a number of factors.



For electrons or electron holes in a solid, the effective mass is usually stated as a factor multiplying the rest mass of an electron, $m_e$ (9.11  10<sup>-31</sup> kg). This factor is usually in the range 0.01 to 10, but can be lower or higherfor example, reaching 1,000 in exotic heavy fermion materials, or anywhere from zero to infinity. The effective mass is an important concept in solid-state physics as it allows us to understand the behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice and predict the electronic properties of materials.



### Subsection: 4.1c Effective Mass and Carrier Mobility



In addition to its role in determining electronic properties, effective mass also plays a crucial role in understanding carrier mobility in materials. Carrier mobility refers to the ability of charge carriers, such as electrons or holes, to move through a material in response to an electric field. The effective mass of these carriers directly affects their mobility, as it determines how easily they can move through the crystal lattice.



In general, materials with a lower effective mass have higher carrier mobility, as the carriers experience less resistance when moving through the lattice. This is why materials such as graphene, with its low effective mass, have such high carrier mobility and are being explored for use in high-speed electronic devices.



The relationship between effective mass and carrier mobility can also be seen in the context of semiconductors. In semiconductors, the effective mass of electrons in the conduction band is typically much lower than the effective mass of holes in the valence band. This results in higher electron mobility compared to hole mobility, which is why electrons are the dominant charge carriers in most semiconductor devices.



In summary, the effective mass of charge carriers in a material plays a crucial role in determining their mobility and ultimately, the electronic properties of the material. By understanding the concept of effective mass, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of electrons in a crystal lattice and how they contribute to the overall properties of a material. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.2 Chemical Potential and Equilibrium



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of effective mass and its importance in solid-state physics. In this section, we will explore the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving equilibrium in a solid-state system.



#### 4.2a Definition of Chemical Potential



Chemical potential, denoted by $\mu$, is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics that describes the energy required to add an extra mole of a substance to a system at constant temperature and pressure. It is a measure of the potential energy of a substance due to its interactions with other particles in the system.



In solid-state physics, the chemical potential is often referred to as the internal chemical potential, as it takes into account the internal factors such as density and temperature, rather than external force fields. It can be calculated using the total energy of a system, which is the sum of the internal energy and the external energy due to interactions with external fields.



The internal chemical potential is related to the total chemical potential, which also includes the contributions from external force fields such as electric potential and gravitational potential. This can be expressed as:


$$

\mu_{tot} = \mu_{int} + \mu_{ext}

$$


where $\mu_{ext}$ is the external chemical potential, which can be further broken down into the contributions from electric potential, gravitational potential, and other external fields.



The concept of chemical potential is particularly important in solid-state applications, as it allows us to understand and predict the behavior of particles in a crystal lattice. By considering the interactions between particles and their surroundings, we can determine the energy required to add or remove particles from a system, and thus achieve equilibrium.



In the next subsection, we will explore the relationship between chemical potential and equilibrium in more detail. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.2 Chemical Potential and Equilibrium



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of effective mass and its importance in solid-state physics. In this section, we will explore the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving equilibrium in a solid-state system.



#### 4.2a Definition of Chemical Potential



Chemical potential, denoted by $\mu$, is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics that describes the energy required to add an extra mole of a substance to a system at constant temperature and pressure. It is a measure of the potential energy of a substance due to its interactions with other particles in the system.



In solid-state physics, the chemical potential is often referred to as the internal chemical potential, as it takes into account the internal factors such as density and temperature, rather than external force fields. It can be calculated using the total energy of a system, which is the sum of the internal energy and the external energy due to interactions with external fields.



The internal chemical potential is related to the total chemical potential, which also includes the contributions from external force fields such as electric potential and gravitational potential. This can be expressed as:


$$

\mu_{tot} = \mu_{int} + \mu_{ext}

$$


where $\mu_{ext}$ is the external chemical potential, which can be further broken down into the contributions from electric potential, gravitational potential, and other external fields.



The concept of chemical potential is particularly important in solid-state applications, as it allows us to understand and predict the behavior of particles in a crystal lattice. By considering the interactions between particles and their surroundings, we can determine the energy required to add or remove particles from a system, and thus achieve equilibrium.



In the next subsection, we will explore the relationship between chemical potential and equilibrium in more detail.



#### 4.2b Fermi-Dirac Distribution



In the previous section, we discussed the Fermi-Dirac distribution, which describes the average number of fermions in a single-particle state in a system of identical fermions in thermodynamic equilibrium. This distribution is dependent on the chemical potential, temperature, and energy of the single-particle state.



At zero absolute temperature, the chemical potential is equal to the Fermi energy plus the potential energy per fermion, provided it is in a neighborhood of positive spectral density. In the case of a spectral gap, such as for electrons in a semiconductor, the chemical potential, also known as the Fermi level or electrochemical potential, will be located in the middle of the gap.



The Fermi-Dirac distribution is only valid if the number of fermions in the system is large enough so that adding one more fermion to the system has a negligible effect on the chemical potential. This is due to the Pauli exclusion principle, which states that at most one fermion can occupy each possible state.



From the Fermi-Dirac distribution, we can also determine the distribution of particles over energy. By multiplying the distribution by the degeneracy of the energy state, we can find the average number of fermions with a specific energy. This is particularly useful in understanding the behavior of particles in a solid-state system.



In the next section, we will explore the concept of equilibrium in more detail and how the chemical potential plays a crucial role in achieving it.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.2 Chemical Potential and Equilibrium



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of effective mass and its importance in solid-state physics. In this section, we will explore the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving equilibrium in a solid-state system.



#### 4.2a Definition of Chemical Potential



Chemical potential, denoted by $\mu$, is a fundamental concept in thermodynamics that describes the energy required to add an extra mole of a substance to a system at constant temperature and pressure. It is a measure of the potential energy of a substance due to its interactions with other particles in the system.



In solid-state physics, the chemical potential is often referred to as the internal chemical potential, as it takes into account the internal factors such as density and temperature, rather than external force fields. It can be calculated using the total energy of a system, which is the sum of the internal energy and the external energy due to interactions with external fields.



The internal chemical potential is related to the total chemical potential, which also includes the contributions from external force fields such as electric potential and gravitational potential. This can be expressed as:


$$

\mu_{tot} = \mu_{int} + \mu_{ext}

$$


where $\mu_{ext}$ is the external chemical potential, which can be further broken down into the contributions from electric potential, gravitational potential, and other external fields.



The concept of chemical potential is particularly important in solid-state applications, as it allows us to understand and predict the behavior of particles in a crystal lattice. By considering the interactions between particles and their surroundings, we can determine the energy required to add or remove particles from a system, and thus achieve equilibrium.



In the next subsection, we will explore the relationship between chemical potential and temperature, and how it affects the equilibrium state of a solid-state system.



#### 4.2b Temperature Dependence of Chemical Potential



The chemical potential of a substance is not a constant value, but rather depends on various factors such as temperature, pressure, and composition. In this subsection, we will focus on the temperature dependence of chemical potential.



As temperature increases, the internal energy of a system also increases, leading to a higher chemical potential. This is because at higher temperatures, particles have more thermal energy and are more likely to overcome the potential barriers and interact with other particles. This results in a higher energy requirement to add an extra mole of a substance to the system, and thus a higher chemical potential.



However, the temperature dependence of chemical potential is not linear. In fact, it follows a logarithmic relationship, as shown by the following equation:


$$

\mu(T) = \mu_0 + kT\ln\left(\frac{T}{T_0}\right)

$$


where $\mu_0$ is the chemical potential at a reference temperature $T_0$, and $k$ is the Boltzmann constant.



This logarithmic relationship can be explained by the fact that at higher temperatures, the thermal energy of particles increases exponentially, resulting in a larger spread of energy levels. This leads to a larger number of available states for particles to occupy, and thus a higher chemical potential.



The temperature dependence of chemical potential is particularly important in solid-state applications, as it affects the equilibrium state of a system. For example, in a semiconductor material, the chemical potential determines the concentration of charge carriers and thus the electrical conductivity. By controlling the temperature, we can manipulate the chemical potential and achieve the desired equilibrium state for specific applications.



In the next subsection, we will explore the effects of pressure and ionic strength on the chemical potential and equilibrium state of a solid-state system.



#### 4.2c Pressure and Ionic Strength Dependence of Chemical Potential



In addition to temperature, the chemical potential of a substance is also affected by pressure and ionic strength. Pressure can affect the chemical potential by altering the intermolecular distances and thus the potential energy of interactions between particles. Similarly, ionic strength, which is a measure of the concentration of ions in a solution, can affect the chemical potential by altering the electrostatic interactions between particles.



The dependence of chemical potential on pressure and ionic strength has been extensively studied, and it has been found that the value of chemical potential decreases with increasing pressure and ionic strength. This is because at higher pressures and ionic strengths, the interactions between particles become stronger, resulting in a lower energy requirement to add an extra mole of a substance to the system.



In solid-state applications, the effects of pressure and ionic strength on chemical potential are particularly important in understanding and predicting the behavior of materials in different environments. By controlling these factors, we can manipulate the chemical potential and achieve the desired equilibrium state for specific applications.



In the next section, we will explore the concept of chemical potential in the context of specific solid-state applications, such as vapor pressures and self-ionization of water. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.3 Chemical Potential and Non-equilibrium



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of effective mass and its importance in solid-state physics. In this section, we will explore the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving non-equilibrium in a solid-state system.



#### 4.3a Non-equilibrium Carrier Distributions



In solid-state applications, it is often necessary to consider non-equilibrium carrier distributions in order to understand and predict the behavior of particles in a crystal lattice. Non-equilibrium carrier distributions occur when the system is not in thermal equilibrium, meaning that the temperature and chemical potential are not constant throughout the system.



One example of non-equilibrium carrier distributions is in the operation of a step recovery diode (SRD). SRDs are semiconductor devices that are used in high-speed switching applications. They work by exploiting the non-equilibrium carrier distributions that occur when a reverse-bias voltage is applied to the diode. This creates a high electric field in the depletion region, causing a large number of carriers to be injected into the conduction band. When the reverse-bias voltage is removed, the carriers are swept out of the depletion region, creating a fast switching action.



Another example of non-equilibrium carrier distributions is in the use of Monte Carlo methods for electron transport. These methods are used to simulate the behavior of electrons in a solid-state system, taking into account the effects of scattering and non-equilibrium conditions. By using Monte Carlo simulations, researchers can gain a better understanding of the behavior of electrons in different materials and structures, and use this knowledge to design more efficient devices.



In order to accurately model non-equilibrium carrier distributions, it is important to consider the chemical potential of the system. The chemical potential is a measure of the energy required to add or remove particles from a system, and it plays a crucial role in achieving equilibrium. In non-equilibrium conditions, the chemical potential can vary throughout the system, leading to non-uniform carrier distributions.



In the next subsection, we will discuss the comparison of semiclassical models for non-equilibrium carrier distributions and their accuracy in predicting the behavior of particles in solid-state systems. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.3 Chemical Potential and Non-equilibrium



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of effective mass and its importance in solid-state physics. In this section, we will explore the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving non-equilibrium in a solid-state system.



#### 4.3a Non-equilibrium Carrier Distributions



In solid-state applications, it is often necessary to consider non-equilibrium carrier distributions in order to understand and predict the behavior of particles in a crystal lattice. Non-equilibrium carrier distributions occur when the system is not in thermal equilibrium, meaning that the temperature and chemical potential are not constant throughout the system.



One example of non-equilibrium carrier distributions is in the operation of a step recovery diode (SRD). SRDs are semiconductor devices that are used in high-speed switching applications. They work by exploiting the non-equilibrium carrier distributions that occur when a reverse-bias voltage is applied to the diode. This creates a high electric field in the depletion region, causing a large number of carriers to be injected into the conduction band. When the reverse-bias voltage is removed, the carriers are swept out of the depletion region, creating a fast switching action.



Another example of non-equilibrium carrier distributions is in the use of Monte Carlo methods for electron transport. These methods are used to simulate the behavior of electrons in a solid-state system, taking into account the effects of scattering and non-equilibrium conditions. By using Monte Carlo simulations, researchers can gain a better understanding of the behavior of electrons in different materials and structures, and use this knowledge to design more efficient devices.



In order to accurately model non-equilibrium carrier distributions, it is important to consider the chemical potential of the system. The chemical potential, denoted by the symbol $\mu$, is a measure of the energy required to add or remove a particle from a system. In a solid-state system, the chemical potential is affected by factors such as temperature, pressure, and the presence of impurities.



#### 4.3b Relaxation Time Approximation



In order to understand the behavior of non-equilibrium carrier distributions, we can use the relaxation time approximation. This approximation assumes that the system is in a steady state, meaning that the distribution of particles does not change over time. It also assumes that the particles are in thermal equilibrium with their surroundings, but the system as a whole is not in thermal equilibrium.



The relaxation time approximation is based on the Boltzmann transport equation, which describes the evolution of the distribution of particles in a system. By solving this equation, we can determine the distribution of particles at any given time. However, this equation is difficult to solve analytically, so the relaxation time approximation is often used as a simplification.



The relaxation time approximation assumes that the distribution of particles can be described by a Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution, which is a function of the particle's energy and the temperature of the system. This distribution is then modified by a relaxation time, denoted by the symbol $\tau$, which represents the average time between collisions for a particle in the system.



Using this approximation, we can calculate the non-equilibrium carrier distribution in a solid-state system and gain a better understanding of its behavior. This is particularly useful in the design and optimization of electronic devices, where non-equilibrium conditions can greatly affect their performance.



In conclusion, the concept of chemical potential and the relaxation time approximation are important tools in understanding non-equilibrium carrier distributions in solid-state systems. By considering these factors, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of particles in materials and use this knowledge to improve the performance of electronic devices.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.3 Chemical Potential and Non-equilibrium



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of effective mass and its importance in solid-state physics. In this section, we will explore the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving non-equilibrium in a solid-state system.



#### 4.3a Non-equilibrium Carrier Distributions



In solid-state applications, it is often necessary to consider non-equilibrium carrier distributions in order to understand and predict the behavior of particles in a crystal lattice. Non-equilibrium carrier distributions occur when the system is not in thermal equilibrium, meaning that the temperature and chemical potential are not constant throughout the system.



One example of non-equilibrium carrier distributions is in the operation of a step recovery diode (SRD). SRDs are semiconductor devices that are used in high-speed switching applications. They work by exploiting the non-equilibrium carrier distributions that occur when a reverse-bias voltage is applied to the diode. This creates a high electric field in the depletion region, causing a large number of carriers to be injected into the conduction band. When the reverse-bias voltage is removed, the carriers are swept out of the depletion region, creating a fast switching action.



Another example of non-equilibrium carrier distributions is in the use of Monte Carlo methods for electron transport. These methods are used to simulate the behavior of electrons in a solid-state system, taking into account the effects of scattering and non-equilibrium conditions. By using Monte Carlo simulations, researchers can gain a better understanding of the behavior of electrons in different materials and structures, and use this knowledge to design more efficient devices.



In order to accurately model non-equilibrium carrier distributions, it is important to consider the chemical potential of the system. The chemical potential, denoted by $\mu$, is a thermodynamic quantity that represents the energy required to add or remove a particle from a system. In a solid-state system, the chemical potential is affected by factors such as temperature, pressure, and the number of particles present.



In the case of non-equilibrium conditions, the chemical potential is not constant throughout the system. This is because the system is not in thermal equilibrium, and there may be variations in temperature and pressure. These variations can lead to a non-uniform distribution of particles, resulting in a non-equilibrium state.



#### 4.3b Boltzmann Transport Equation



To accurately describe the behavior of particles in a non-equilibrium state, we can use the Boltzmann Transport Equation (BTE). This equation, derived from the Boltzmann equation, takes into account the effects of scattering and non-equilibrium conditions on the distribution of particles in a solid-state system.



The BTE is a partial differential equation that describes the evolution of the distribution function, $f(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{k}, t)$, which represents the probability of finding a particle at position $\mathbf{r}$ with momentum $\mathbf{k}$ at time $t$. It takes into account the effects of external forces, collisions, and changes in the chemical potential.


$$

{\partial f\over{\partial t}} + \mathbf{v}\cdot\nabla_{\mathbf{r}}f + \mathbf{F}\cdot\nabla_{\mathbf{k}}f = \left({\partial f\over{\partial t}}\right)_{coll}

$$


The first term on the left-hand side represents the change in the distribution function due to the motion of particles, while the second term represents the change due to external forces. The right-hand side represents the effects of collisions, which can cause particles to scatter and change their momentum.



The BTE is a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of particles in a non-equilibrium state. By solving this equation, we can gain insights into the transport properties of materials and design more efficient devices for solid-state applications.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.4 Inhomogeneous Solids



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving non-equilibrium in a solid-state system. In this section, we will explore the effects of inhomogeneity on carrier distributions in solids.



#### 4.4a Carrier Gradients in Solids



Inhomogeneous solids are materials that have variations in their composition or structure on a microscopic scale. These variations can lead to carrier gradients, where the concentration of carriers varies throughout the material. This can have significant effects on the behavior of particles in the solid.



One example of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids is in the operation of a heterojunction. A heterojunction is a junction between two different semiconductor materials. Due to the difference in band structures between the two materials, there is a built-in electric field at the junction. This electric field can cause a gradient in the carrier concentration, with more carriers on one side of the junction than the other. This gradient can affect the transport of carriers across the junction and can be manipulated to control the behavior of the device.



Another example of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids is in the use of quantum wells. Quantum wells are structures that confine carriers in a thin layer, creating a potential well. The carrier concentration in the well can vary depending on the thickness of the layer, leading to carrier gradients. These gradients can affect the energy levels of the carriers and can be used to tune the properties of the device.



In order to accurately model the behavior of carriers in inhomogeneous solids, it is important to consider the effects of carrier gradients. This can be achieved through the use of numerical methods, such as finite element analysis, which can take into account the variations in carrier concentration throughout the material.



Understanding the effects of inhomogeneity on carrier distributions is crucial for the design and optimization of solid-state devices. By considering carrier gradients, researchers can gain a better understanding of the behavior of particles in these materials and use this knowledge to develop more efficient and advanced technologies.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.4 Inhomogeneous Solids



In the previous section, we discussed the concept of chemical potential and its role in achieving non-equilibrium in a solid-state system. In this section, we will explore the effects of inhomogeneity on carrier distributions in solids.



#### 4.4a Carrier Gradients in Solids



Inhomogeneous solids are materials that have variations in their composition or structure on a microscopic scale. These variations can lead to carrier gradients, where the concentration of carriers varies throughout the material. This can have significant effects on the behavior of particles in the solid.



One example of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids is in the operation of a heterojunction. A heterojunction is a junction between two different semiconductor materials. Due to the difference in band structures between the two materials, there is a built-in electric field at the junction. This electric field can cause a gradient in the carrier concentration, with more carriers on one side of the junction than the other. This gradient can affect the transport of carriers across the junction and can be manipulated to control the behavior of the device.



Another example of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids is in the use of quantum wells. Quantum wells are structures that confine carriers in a thin layer, creating a potential well. The carrier concentration in the well can vary depending on the thickness of the layer, leading to carrier gradients. These gradients can affect the energy levels of the carriers and can be used to tune the properties of the device.



In order to accurately model the behavior of carriers in inhomogeneous solids, it is important to consider the effects of carrier gradients. This can be achieved through the use of numerical methods, such as finite element analysis, which can take into account the variations in carrier concentration throughout the material. However, in order to fully understand the behavior of carriers in inhomogeneous solids, we must also consider the effects of diffusion and drift currents.



### Subsection: 4.4b Diffusion and Drift Currents



In inhomogeneous solids, the presence of carrier gradients can lead to the movement of carriers through two main mechanisms: diffusion and drift currents. Diffusion is the random movement of particles from an area of high concentration to an area of low concentration, while drift currents are caused by the presence of an electric field.



To understand the behavior of diffusion and drift currents in inhomogeneous solids, we can start by looking at the generalized Ohm's law and the curl equations for small displacement currents. By substituting the current density into the Ampere-Maxwell law, we can derive an expression for the electric field in terms of the magnetic field and the velocity of the carriers. Taking the curl of this equation and substituting it into Faraday's law, we can simplify the expression to eliminate cross products and write it in terms of the "i"-th component of the magnetic field and the Levi-Cevita tensor. This allows us to see the effects of carrier gradients on the movement of carriers in inhomogeneous solids.



In order to accurately model the behavior of carriers in inhomogeneous solids, we must also take into account the effects of carrier scattering and recombination. These processes can affect the movement of carriers and can be influenced by the presence of carrier gradients. By considering all of these factors, we can gain a better understanding of the behavior of carriers in inhomogeneous solids and use this knowledge to design and optimize solid-state devices for various applications.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.4 Inhomogeneous Solids



In the previous section, we discussed the effects of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids. In this section, we will explore the continuity equation and carrier recombination in these materials.



#### 4.4b Continuity Equation



The continuity equation is a fundamental equation in solid-state physics that describes the conservation of charge in a material. It states that the rate of change of charge density in a given region is equal to the net current flowing into or out of that region. In mathematical terms, it can be written as:


$$

\frac{\partial \rho}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot \mathbf{J} = 0

$$


where $\rho$ is the charge density and $\mathbf{J}$ is the current density.



In the case of inhomogeneous solids, the continuity equation takes on a more complex form. The presence of carrier gradients leads to variations in the charge density, which must be taken into account. Additionally, the presence of an electric field can also affect the charge density, as carriers may be pushed or pulled in a certain direction.



#### 4.4c Carrier Recombination



In inhomogeneous solids, the presence of carrier gradients can also affect the process of carrier recombination. Carrier recombination is the process by which electrons and holes combine, resulting in the release of energy in the form of photons or heat. In equilibrium, the rate of carrier recombination is equal to the rate of carrier generation, resulting in a steady-state carrier concentration.



However, in inhomogeneous solids, the presence of carrier gradients can lead to variations in the rate of carrier recombination. This is due to the fact that the recombination rate is dependent on the carrier concentration, which may vary throughout the material. Additionally, the presence of an electric field can also affect the recombination rate, as it can alter the movement of carriers and their likelihood of recombining.



To accurately model the effects of carrier gradients on carrier recombination, it is important to consider the local carrier concentration and electric field in the material. This can be achieved through the use of numerical methods, such as finite element analysis, which can take into account the variations in carrier concentration and electric field throughout the material.



In conclusion, the continuity equation and carrier recombination play important roles in understanding the behavior of inhomogeneous solids. By considering the effects of carrier gradients and electric fields, we can gain a better understanding of how carriers behave in these materials and how they can be manipulated for various applications. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.5 Scattering of Bloch Functions



In the previous section, we discussed the effects of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids. In this section, we will explore the scattering of Bloch functions, which is an important phenomenon in solid-state physics.



#### 4.5a Scattering Mechanisms in Solids



Scattering of Bloch functions refers to the process of electrons being scattered by impurities, defects, or phonons in a solid. This scattering can lead to a decrease in the mobility of carriers, which in turn affects the electrical conductivity of the material.



There are several different scattering mechanisms that can occur in solids. One of the most common is carrier-impurity scattering, where electrons are scattered by impurities in the crystal lattice. This type of scattering is described by the Matthiessen's rule, which states that the total scattering rate is the sum of the individual scattering rates from each type of scattering mechanism.



Another important scattering mechanism is carrier-phonon scattering, where electrons are scattered by lattice vibrations, or phonons. This type of scattering is described by Fermi's golden rule, which relates the scattering rate to the interaction matrix between the electron and phonon states.



### Subsection: 4.5b Scattering Rate for Acoustic Phonons



Using Fermi's golden rule, the scattering rate for low energy acoustic phonons can be approximated. The interaction matrix for these phonons is given by:


$$

|<k'|\widehat{H}_{int}|k>|^{2}=Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (N_{q}+\frac{1}{2}\pm \frac{1}{2})\delta _{k', k \pm q} \; \; (15)

$$


where $\omega_{q}$ is the phonon angular frequency, V is the volume of the solid, $\rho$ is the solid density, and c is the phonon group velocity. Plugging this into Eq. 6 gives:


$$

S_{k'k}^{Ac}=\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (N_{q}+\frac{1}{2}\pm \frac{1}{2})\delta _{k', k \pm q}\delta [E(k')-E(k) \pm \hbar \omega _{q}] \; \; (16)

$$


With the assumptions that $N_{q} \gg 1$, $\hbar \omega \ll kT$, and $g(E') \sim g(E)$, which generally holds for 3D crystals since conduction electron energies are much greater than $\hbar \omega$ and $g(E)$ lacks any van Hove singularity, the scattering rate can be simplified to:


$$

\frac{1}{\tau} = \sum_{k'} S_{k'k}^{Ac}=\sum_{k} S_{k\pm q ,k}^{Ac} =\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (\frac{kT}{\hbar \omega _{q}}) \sum_{k} \delta _{k', k \pm q}\delta [E(k')-E(k) \pm \hbar \omega _{q}] =\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{kT}{2V\rho c^{2}} V \times g(E) =\frac{\sqrt 2}{\pi}\frac{Z_{DP}^{2} m^{*\frac{3}{2}}kT}{\rho \hbar ^{4}c^{2}} \sqrt{E-E_{CB}} \; \; (17)

$$


where $g(E)$ is the electronic density of states, for which the 3-dimensional solution with parabolic dispersion was used to obtain the final answer.



This scattering rate can then be used to calculate the mobility of carriers in the material, which is an important factor in determining the overall electrical conductivity. By understanding the scattering mechanisms and their effects on the mobility of carriers, we can gain a better understanding of the electrical properties of solid-state materials.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.5 Scattering of Bloch Functions



In the previous section, we discussed the effects of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids. In this section, we will explore the scattering of Bloch functions, which is an important phenomenon in solid-state physics.



#### 4.5a Scattering Mechanisms in Solids



Scattering of Bloch functions refers to the process of electrons being scattered by impurities, defects, or phonons in a solid. This scattering can lead to a decrease in the mobility of carriers, which in turn affects the electrical conductivity of the material.



There are several different scattering mechanisms that can occur in solids. One of the most common is carrier-impurity scattering, where electrons are scattered by impurities in the crystal lattice. This type of scattering is described by the Matthiessen's rule, which states that the total scattering rate is the sum of the individual scattering rates from each type of scattering mechanism.



Another important scattering mechanism is carrier-phonon scattering, where electrons are scattered by lattice vibrations, or phonons. This type of scattering is described by Fermi's golden rule, which relates the scattering rate to the interaction matrix between the electron and phonon states.



### Subsection: 4.5b Impurity and Lattice Scattering



In addition to carrier-impurity and carrier-phonon scattering, there are other scattering mechanisms that can occur in solids. These include defect scattering, where electrons are scattered by defects in the crystal lattice, and electron-electron scattering, where electrons are scattered by other electrons.



The total scattering rate in a solid can be described by the Matthiessen's rule, which states that the total scattering rate is the sum of the individual scattering rates from each type of scattering mechanism. This rule is based on the assumption that the scattering mechanisms are independent of each other.



However, this assumption is not always valid. In some cases, the scattering mechanisms may be dependent on each other, leading to a violation of Matthiessen's rule. In these cases, more complex formulas must be used to accurately describe the scattering rate.



### Subsection: 4.5c Scattering Rate for Acoustic Phonons



Using Fermi's golden rule, the scattering rate for low energy acoustic phonons can be approximated. The interaction matrix for these phonons is given by:


$$

|<k'|\widehat{H}_{int}|k>|^{2}=Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (N_{q}+\frac{1}{2}\pm \frac{1}{2})\delta _{k', k \pm q} \; \; (15)

$$


where $\omega_{q}$ is the phonon angular frequency, V is the volume of the solid, $\rho$ is the solid density, and c is the phonon group velocity. Plugging this into Eq. 6 gives:


$$

S_{k'k}^{Ac}=\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (N_{q}+\frac{1}{2}\pm \frac{1}{2})\delta _{k', k \pm q} \; \; (16)

$$


where $S_{k'k}^{Ac}$ is the scattering rate for acoustic phonons. This equation shows that the scattering rate is proportional to the phonon density of states and the square of the electron-phonon coupling strength.



In general, the scattering rate for phonons can be written as:


$$

S_{k'k}^{ph}=\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} \sum_{q} |<k'|\widehat{H}_{int}|k>|^{2} (N_{q}+\frac{1}{2}\pm \frac{1}{2})\delta _{k', k \pm q} \; \; (17)

$$


where the sum is taken over all phonon modes. This equation shows that the scattering rate for phonons is dependent on the electron-phonon coupling strength and the phonon occupation number.



In conclusion, the scattering of Bloch functions is an important phenomenon in solid-state physics that can significantly affect the mobility of carriers and the electrical conductivity of materials. By understanding the different scattering mechanisms and using appropriate equations, we can accurately describe the scattering rate and its effects on the behavior of electrons in solids.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.5 Scattering of Bloch Functions



In the previous section, we discussed the effects of carrier gradients in inhomogeneous solids. In this section, we will explore the scattering of Bloch functions, which is an important phenomenon in solid-state physics.



#### 4.5a Scattering Mechanisms in Solids



Scattering of Bloch functions refers to the process of electrons being scattered by impurities, defects, or phonons in a solid. This scattering can lead to a decrease in the mobility of carriers, which in turn affects the electrical conductivity of the material.



There are several different scattering mechanisms that can occur in solids. One of the most common is carrier-impurity scattering, where electrons are scattered by impurities in the crystal lattice. This type of scattering is described by the Matthiessen's rule, which states that the total scattering rate is the sum of the individual scattering rates from each type of scattering mechanism.



Another important scattering mechanism is carrier-phonon scattering, where electrons are scattered by lattice vibrations, or phonons. This type of scattering is described by Fermi's golden rule, which relates the scattering rate to the interaction matrix between the electron and phonon states.



### Subsection: 4.5b Impurity and Lattice Scattering



In addition to carrier-impurity and carrier-phonon scattering, there are other scattering mechanisms that can occur in solids. These include defect scattering, where electrons are scattered by defects in the crystal lattice, and electron-electron scattering, where electrons are scattered by other electrons.



The total scattering rate in a solid can be described by the Matthiessen's rule, which states that the total scattering rate is the sum of the individual scattering rates from each type of scattering mechanism. This rule is based on the assumption that the scattering mechanisms are independent of each other and do not interact. However, in reality, there can be interactions between different scattering mechanisms, leading to a more complex scattering process.



### Subsection: 4.5c Scattering Rates and Relaxation Time



The scattering rate, denoted by <math>\Gamma</math>, is a measure of how often an electron is scattered by a particular mechanism. It is defined as the inverse of the average time between scattering events. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:


$$

\Gamma = \frac{1}{\tau}

$$


where <math>\tau</math> is the average time between scattering events, also known as the relaxation time. The relaxation time is an important parameter in solid-state physics as it determines the mobility of carriers and the electrical conductivity of the material.



The relaxation time can be calculated using the scattering rate and the density of states, <math>D(E)</math>, which represents the number of available states at a particular energy level. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:


$$

\tau = \frac{1}{\Gamma} = \frac{1}{2\pi\hbar} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} D(E) \left|V_{if}\right|^2 \delta(E-E_f) \delta(E-E_i) dE

$$


where <math>V_{if}</math> is the interaction matrix element between the initial and final states, and <math>E_i</math> and <math>E_f</math> are the initial and final energy levels, respectively.



The relaxation time is an important concept in understanding the behavior of carriers in solids. It is affected by various factors such as temperature, impurity concentration, and crystal structure. In general, a longer relaxation time leads to a higher carrier mobility and a more conductive material.



In conclusion, the scattering of Bloch functions plays a crucial role in determining the properties of solids. By understanding the different scattering mechanisms and their effects on the relaxation time, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of carriers in solid-state applications. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.6 Electron-Phonon Scattering



In the previous section, we discussed the scattering of Bloch functions in solids. In this section, we will focus on a specific type of scattering mechanism, namely electron-phonon scattering.



#### 4.6a Interaction of Electrons and Phonons



Electron-phonon scattering is the process in which an electron interacts with a phonon, a quantized lattice vibration, in a solid. This interaction can lead to a decrease in the mobility of carriers, affecting the electrical conductivity of the material.



To understand this phenomenon, we must first examine the displacement operator of the longitudinal acoustic (LA) phonon. The equations of motion for the atoms in a periodic lattice can be described by the displacement <math>u_{n}</math> of the "n"th atom from its equilibrium position. This displacement can be expressed as <math>u_{\ell}= x_{\ell} - \ell a</math>, where <math>x_{\ell}</math> is the coordinates of the <math>\ell</math>th atom and <math>a</math> is the lattice constant.



Using Fourier transform, we can express the displacement as <math>u_{\ell}= A e^{i ( q \ell a - \omega t)}</math>, where <math>q</math> is the wave vector and <math>\omega</math> is the angular frequency of the phonon. This allows us to define the displacement operator for the 3-dimensional case as <math>u_{\ell}= A e^{i ( \mathbf{q} \cdot \mathbf{r} - \omega t)}</math>, where <math>\mathbf{q}</math> is the wave vector and <math>\mathbf{r}</math> is the position vector.



The interaction between an electron and a phonon can be described by the electron-longitudinal acoustic phonon interaction Hamiltonian, <math>H_\text{el}</math>. This Hamiltonian includes the deformation potential for electron scattering by acoustic phonons, <math>D_\text{ac}</math>. When we insert the displacement vector into the Hamiltonian, we get <math>H_\text{el}= D_\text{ac} \mathbf{e_{q}} \cdot \mathbf{r} </math>, where <math>\mathbf{e_{q}}</math> is the unit vector along the displacement direction.



Using Fermi's golden rule, we can calculate the scattering probability for an electron to transition from state <math>|k \rangle </math> to state <math>|k' \rangle</math>. This probability is given by <math>P_{k,k'}= \frac{2\pi}{\hbar} | \langle k' | H_\text{el} | k \rangle |^2 \delta(E_{k'}-E_{k})</math>, where <math>E_{k}</math> and <math>E_{k'}</math> are the energies of the initial and final states, respectively.



To simplify this expression, we can replace the integral over the whole space with a summation of unit cell integrations, resulting in <math>P_{k,k'}= \frac{2\pi}{\hbar} | \langle k' | H_\text{el} | k \rangle |^2 I(k,k') \delta(E_{k'}-E_{k})</math>, where <math>I(k,k') = \Omega \int_{\Omega} d^{3}r \, u^{\ast}_{k'} (r) u_{k} (r) </math> and <math> \Omega </math> is the volume of a unit cell.



In conclusion, electron-phonon scattering is an important phenomenon in solid-state physics that can significantly affect the electrical conductivity of a material. By understanding the interaction between electrons and phonons, we can better understand the behavior of carriers in solids and their impact on electronic devices.





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.6 Electron-Phonon Scattering



In the previous section, we discussed the scattering of Bloch functions in solids. In this section, we will focus on a specific type of scattering mechanism, namely electron-phonon scattering.



#### 4.6a Interaction of Electrons and Phonons



Electron-phonon scattering is a fundamental process that plays a crucial role in determining the transport properties of materials. It is the process in which an electron interacts with a phonon, a quantized lattice vibration, in a solid. This interaction can lead to a decrease in the mobility of carriers, affecting the electrical conductivity of the material.



To understand this phenomenon, we must first examine the displacement operator of the longitudinal acoustic (LA) phonon. The equations of motion for the atoms in a periodic lattice can be described by the displacement $u_{n}$ of the "n"th atom from its equilibrium position. This displacement can be expressed as $u_{\ell}= x_{\ell} - \ell a$, where $x_{\ell}$ is the coordinates of the $\ell$th atom and $a$ is the lattice constant.



Using Fourier transform, we can express the displacement as $u_{\ell}= A e^{i ( q \ell a - \omega t)}$, where $q$ is the wave vector and $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the phonon. This allows us to define the displacement operator for the 3-dimensional case as $u_{\ell}= A e^{i ( \mathbf{q} \cdot \mathbf{r} - \omega t)}$, where $\mathbf{q}$ is the wave vector and $\mathbf{r}$ is the position vector.



The interaction between an electron and a phonon can be described by the electron-longitudinal acoustic phonon interaction Hamiltonian, $H_\text{el}$. This Hamiltonian includes the deformation potential for electron scattering by acoustic phonons, $D_\text{ac}$. When we insert the displacement vector into the Hamiltonian, we get $H_\text{el}= D_\text{ac} \mathbf{e_{q}} \cdot \mathbf{u_{q}}$, where $\mathbf{e_{q}}$ is the polarization vector and $\mathbf{u_{q}}$ is the displacement vector.



### Subsection: 4.6b Electron-Phonon Scattering Rates



Now that we have established the interaction between electrons and phonons, we can calculate the scattering rates for this process. Using Fermi's golden rule, the scattering rate for low energy acoustic phonons can be approximated. The interaction matrix for these phonons is $|<k'|\widehat{H}_{int}|k>|^{2}=Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (N_{q}+\frac{1}{2}\pm \frac{1}{2})\delta _{k', k \pm q} \; \; (15)$ with the phonon angular frequency $\omega_{q}=cq$, volume $V$, solid density $\rho$, and phonon group velocity $c$. Plugging this into Eq. 6 gives $S_{k'k}^{Ac}=\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (N_{q}+\frac{1}{2}\pm \frac{1}{2})\delta _{k', k \pm q}\delta [E(k')-E(k) \pm \hbar \omega _{q}] \; \; (16)$. With the assumptions that $N_{q}>>1$, $\hbar \omega <<kT$ and $g(E') \approx g(E)$ (which generally holds for 3D crystals since conduction electron energies are generally much greater than $\hbar \omega$ and $g(E)$ lacks any van Hove singularity), we can simplify the scattering rate to $\frac{1}{\tau} = \sum_{k'} S_{k'k}^{Ac}=\sum_{k} S_{k\pm q ,k}^{Ac} =\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{\hbar \omega _{q}}{2V\rho c^{2}} (\frac{kT}{\hbar \omega _{q}}) \sum_{k} \delta _{k', k \pm q}\delta [E(k')-E(k) \pm \hbar \omega _{q}] =\frac{2\pi}{\hbar} Z_{DP}^{2}\frac{kT}{2V\rho c^{2}} V \times g(E) =\frac{\sqrt 2}{\pi}\frac{Z_{DP}^{2} m^{*\frac{3}{2}}kT}{\rho \hbar ^{4}c^{2}} \sqrt{E-E_{CB}} \; \; (17)$ where $g(E)$ is the electronic density of states for which the 3-dimensional solution with parabolic dispersion was used to obtain the final answer.



This scattering rate is crucial in understanding the transport properties of materials. It is affected by various factors such as temperature, phonon frequency, and electron density. By studying the electron-phonon scattering rates, we can gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of carriers in solids and how they contribute to the overall properties of the material. 





# Physics for Solid-State Applications



## Chapter 4: Effective Mass and Equilibrium



### Section: 4.6 Electron-Phonon Scattering



In the previous section, we discussed the scattering of Bloch functions in solids. In this section, we will focus on a specific type of scattering mechanism, namely electron-phonon scattering.



#### 4.6a Interaction of Electrons and Phonons



Electron-phonon scattering is a fundamental process that plays a crucial role in determining the transport properties of materials. It is the process in which an electron interacts with a phonon, a quantized lattice vibration, in a solid. This interaction can lead to a decrease in the mobility of carriers, affecting the electrical conductivity of the material.



To understand this phenomenon, we must first examine the displacement operator of the longitudinal acoustic (LA) phonon. The equations of motion for the atoms in a periodic lattice can be described by the displacement $u_{n}$ of the "n"th atom from its equilibrium position. This displacement can be expressed as $u_{\ell}= x_{\ell} - \ell a$, where $x_{\ell}$ is the coordinates of the $\ell$th atom and $a$ is the lattice constant.



Using Fourier transform, we can express the displacement as $u_{\ell}= A e^{i ( q \ell a - \omega t)}$, where $q$ is the wave vector and $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the phonon. This allows us to define the displacement operator for the 3-dimensional case as $u_{\ell}= A e^{i ( \mathbf{q} \cdot \mathbf{r} - \omega t)}$, where $\mathbf{q}$ is the wave vector and $\mathbf{r}$ is the position vector.



The interaction between an electron and a phonon can be described by the electron-longitudinal acoustic phonon interaction Hamiltonian, $H_\text{el}$. This Hamiltonian includes the deformation potential for electron scattering by acoustic phonons, $D_\text{ac}$. When we insert the displacement vector into the Hamiltonian, we get $H_\text{el}= D_\text{ac} \mathbf{e_{q}} \cdot \mathbf{u_{q}}$, where $\mathbf{e_{q}}$ is the polarization vector of the phonon.



### 4.6b Electron-Phonon Scattering Mechanisms



There are two main mechanisms of electron-phonon scattering: deformation potential scattering and piezoelectric scattering. In deformation potential scattering, the electron is scattered by the deformation potential of the lattice due to the presence of phonons. This type of scattering is dominant at low temperatures and for low-energy phonons.



On the other hand, piezoelectric scattering occurs when the electron is scattered by the electric field generated by the lattice deformation. This type of scattering is dominant at high temperatures and for high-energy phonons.



### 4.6c Influence on Electrical and Thermal Conductivity



Electron-phonon scattering has a significant impact on the electrical and thermal conductivity of materials. As mentioned earlier, the scattering of electrons by phonons can decrease the mobility of carriers, leading to a decrease in electrical conductivity. This effect is more pronounced at low temperatures, where phonon scattering is the dominant mechanism.



In terms of thermal conductivity, electron-phonon scattering can also play a role. When an electron scatters off a phonon, it transfers some of its energy to the phonon, leading to an increase in lattice vibrations and thus an increase in thermal conductivity. However, at high temperatures, this effect is counteracted by the increased phonon-phonon scattering, which decreases thermal conductivity.



In summary, electron-phonon scattering is a crucial process that affects the transport properties of materials. Understanding this phenomenon is essential for designing materials with desired electrical and thermal conductivity properties. 





### Conclusion

In this chapter, we explored the concept of effective mass and its importance in understanding the behavior of electrons in solid-state materials. We learned that effective mass is a measure of how easily an electron can move in a material under the influence of an external force. We also discussed how effective mass can vary depending on the direction of the force and the crystal structure of the material.



We saw that the concept of effective mass is crucial in understanding the equilibrium state of a solid-state material. By analyzing the energy bands and the effective mass of electrons, we can determine the stability and conductivity of a material. This knowledge is essential for the development and application of various solid-state devices, such as transistors and solar cells.



Overall, this chapter has provided us with a deeper understanding of the fundamental principles of solid-state physics. We have learned how the behavior of electrons in materials can be described using effective mass and how it plays a crucial role in the equilibrium state of a material. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for further exploration into the world of solid-state applications.
